    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/669978-i-m-a-teacher-and-do-yoga-and-pilates-i">I&#39;m a teacher and do yoga and Pilates. I would like to start a reading habit to learn better how to meditate and become more aware of self.

Is this like a first step to start or do you recommend any other readings?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_669978'><a id="like_count_community_question_669978" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/669978?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">1 like</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F95747.The_Miracle_of_Mindfulness">like</a></span>

<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/4455202-xavier-guillaume"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1543353173i/4455202._UY100_CR16,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/4455202-xavier-guillaume">Xavier Guillaume</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">If you turned to page 79 there is a chapter on Exercises in Mindfulness. The book describes mindfulness as not just something you do as you meditate, <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">If you turned to page 79 there is a chapter on Exercises in Mindfulness. The book describes mindfulness as not just something you do as you meditate, but something you kind of do throughout the day. So it is like always meditating. It is a lot, but that's why the book breaks it down into little steps. Like being mindful when you wake up, or being mindful when you bathe, or being mindful while you walk, or do dishes, etc. As you practice the exercises you can learn to be mindful throughout the day. But the book also teaches how to meditate too, so that you can go deeper into your understanding of your place in the Universe and the interconnection that is life.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

<div id="review_263770064" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/263770064" />
    <a title="Lisa (Harmonybites)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2596042-lisa-harmonybites"><img alt="Lisa (Harmonybites)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1337396485p2/2596042.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/263770064?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa (Harmonybites)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa (Harmonybites)" href="/user/show/2596042-lisa-harmonybites">Lisa (Harmonybites)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Lisa (Harmonybites) by: </span>
      The Complete Idiot's Guide to the Ultimate Reading List
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2596042-lisa-harmonybites?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2596042-lisa-harmonybites?shelf=ultimate-reading-list">ultimate-reading-list</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2596042-lisa-harmonybites?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2596042-lisa-harmonybites?shelf=reference">reference</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2596042-lisa-harmonybites?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2596042-lisa-harmonybites?shelf=self-help">self-help</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2596042-lisa-harmonybites?shelf=spirituality">spirituality</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer263770064" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11103494586152459622">The subtitle is "an introduction to the practice of meditation." That's a bit misleading. This is a lot more than a value-free manual. The introduction tells us this the main text was originally a long letter from Thich Nhat Hanh to a fellow Buddhist monk in Vietnam in the midst of the war in 1975. Hanh, exiled from Vietnam, worked against the war and was nominated by Martin Luther King for the Nobel Peace Prize. <br/><br/>Translated into English under his supervision by a friend, you can't sever this fro</span>
  <span id="freeText11103494586152459622" style="display:none">The subtitle is "an introduction to the practice of meditation." That's a bit misleading. This is a lot more than a value-free manual. The introduction tells us this the main text was originally a long letter from Thich Nhat Hanh to a fellow Buddhist monk in Vietnam in the midst of the war in 1975. Hanh, exiled from Vietnam, worked against the war and was nominated by Martin Luther King for the Nobel Peace Prize. <br /><br />Translated into English under his supervision by a friend, you can't sever this from its Buddhist context. There's a lot about Buddhist philosophy here--even a discussion about such issues at the "naive" depiction of the faith in Hesse's Siddharta. The last chapter consists of a "Selection of Buddhist Sutras" (which I found impenetrable). The writing is lucid, but even though written in deceptively simple language, a lot of the concepts are pretty sophisticated and I think take repeated reading to really understand. Mind you, this isn't an introduction to Buddhism <i>per se.</i> This isn't the place to find an overview of the religion and the focus is on meditation and "mindfulness." <br /><br />Hanh's concept of meditation and mindfulness doesn't necessarily mean what you do in a lotus position while going "ohm." He means by it living in the moment and fully alert even as you drink tea or wash dishes. "Mindfulness frees us of forgetfulness and dispersion and makes it possible to live fully each minute of life." Not that he doesn't see a place for more formal meditation, and he provides several practical exercises, particularly focusing on the breath. "Our breath is the bridge from out body to our mind... it alone is the tool which can bring them both together."<br /><br />My introduction to meditation actually was in the mandatory Religion class in my Catholic high school. I remember feeling silly as we were directed to go "ohm." Later I'd be reintroduced to the practice when I took Yoga classes. I remember feeling frustrated as I was told to clear my mind of all thought--which I thought impossible. So it was interesting and useful that it's not what Hanh directs. He says rather when you have thoughts during meditation, you <i>acknowledge</i> the thought--or feeling. "The essential thing is not to let any feeling or thought arise without recognizing it in mindfulness, like a palace guard who is aware of every face that passes in the front corridor." <br /><br />It's an interesting and useful book if you're curious about meditation and Buddhism, written clearly and succinctly--the main text of the book is only about a hundred pages. Although to get much out of it means <i>reading</i> with mindfulness--repeatedly, slowly, taking notes--and practicing the exercises. And in that regard, I think it does help to do it with others rather than just try to work through the book by yourself.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11103494586152459622" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1377303833" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1377303833" />
    <a title="Mark Robison" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1432012-mark-robison"><img alt="Mark Robison" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1440272820p2/1432012.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1377303833?book_show_action=true">Aug 29, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark Robison" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark Robison" href="/user/show/1432012-mark-robison">Mark Robison</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1377303833" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7719208587841746259">When I read this 20 years ago, it had a big effect on my life. I decided to read it again, and I remembered all the parts that had been so meaningful before but I didn’t love it. In hindsight, I don’t think I read it mindfully. (Irony alert.) So I read it almost immediately again, and absolutely loved it this time. My favorite parts are when he’s traveling across the U.S. and his friend Jim starts popping pieces of a tangerine in his mouth while discussing their plans. He suggests to Jim he ough</span>
  <span id="freeText7719208587841746259" style="display:none">When I read this 20 years ago, it had a big effect on my life. I decided to read it again, and I remembered all the parts that had been so meaningful before but I didn’t love it. In hindsight, I don’t think I read it mindfully. (Irony alert.) So I read it almost immediately again, and absolutely loved it this time. My favorite parts are when he’s traveling across the U.S. and his friend Jim starts popping pieces of a tangerine in his mouth while discussing their plans. He suggests to Jim he ought to eat the tangerine. “It was as if he hadn’t been eating the tangerine at all. If he had been eating anything, he was ‘eating’ his future plans.” There’s also this: “People usually consider walking on water or in thin air a miracle. But I think the real miracle is not to walk either on water or in thin air, but to walk on earth. Every day we are engaged in a miracle which we don’t even recognize: a blue sky, white clouds, green leaves, the black, curious eyes of a child — our own two eyes. All is a miracle.” My other favorite part is his retelling of a Tolstoy story (he made me a fan of Tolstoy, for which I'll be forever grateful) about when is the best time to do each thing, who are the most important people to work with and what is the important thing to do in any moment. The only part I don’t care for in the book are the very repetitive translations of sutras in the appendix, but they are easily skipped and do show the millennia-old basis for his teachings. Grade: A</span>
  <a data-text-id="7719208587841746259" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer559699155697080260">I am trying to find ways to deal with my anxiety and depression and all the rest, now that I am (once again, and for the final time I think) coming off another failed attempt to go on antidepressants. Mindfulness and meditation have been helping a great deal, though it is still early days for me, and I have to undo a lot of prejudice on my part with respect to the “new age” and “self help” baggage that comes with it. This book was a perfect example of the kind of thing I am looking for. Clear, p</span>
  <span id="freeText559699155697080260" style="display:none">I am trying to find ways to deal with my anxiety and depression and all the rest, now that I am (once again, and for the final time I think) coming off another failed attempt to go on antidepressants. Mindfulness and meditation have been helping a great deal, though it is still early days for me, and I have to undo a lot of prejudice on my part with respect to the “new age” and “self help” baggage that comes with it. This book was a perfect example of the kind of thing I am looking for. Clear, practical, and written from a position of experience and hard-won insight. Includes simple exercises (such as washing the dishes being mindful of the act itself for itself, rather than rushing to get on to the next thing you want to do) that are very helpful for a beginner like me. <br />Highly recommended </span>

<div id="review_3544337652" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3544337652" />
    <a title="Raul Bimenyimana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43664238-raul-bimenyimana"><img alt="Raul Bimenyimana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1466510542p2/43664238.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3544337652?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Raul Bimenyimana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Raul Bimenyimana" href="/user/show/43664238-raul-bimenyimana">Raul Bimenyimana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3544337652" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16973806773908163903">A few weeks ago a friend shared a meditation app. These times have been depressing for me and I found myself more anxious than I normally am, which says a lot, and so after years of uncertainty and suspicion about meditation, I began using the app.<br/><br/>There are three reasons why I looked at meditation distrustfully, the first one being the commercialisation of the practice. The second being that ever since I gave up on religion I avoid a lot of things that would be labelled spiritual. The third bein</span>
  <span id="freeText16973806773908163903" style="display:none">A few weeks ago a friend shared a meditation app. These times have been depressing for me and I found myself more anxious than I normally am, which says a lot, and so after years of uncertainty and suspicion about meditation, I began using the app.<br /><br />There are three reasons why I looked at meditation distrustfully, the first one being the commercialisation of the practice. The second being that ever since I gave up on religion I avoid a lot of things that would be labelled spiritual. The third being my (rightful?) association of meditation with monks; the first time I saw a monk was in an Encyclopedia and it was the famous picture of the burning monk Thich Quang Duc whose protest was frightening for a nine year old boy, and like in many scenarios where we don't understand, I was fascinated and scared of these people who could endure flames to call attention to and denounce injustice and persecution.<br /><br />All this is to say and explain that I held prejudices against this wonderful practice before I encountered it. There was little asked of me physically and financially so I went for it, the app helped, I could focus better, things were less scattered, but it felt incomplete. And then I remembered the name of a famous Vietnamese monk who wrote about meditation and this led me to the first Thich Nhat Hahn book that I've read.<br /><br />This book has been helpful. I believe the teachings here of interdependence, empathy, understanding of the world around us, pacifism, paying attention to our well being are/should be universal. With remarkable gentleness, grief, death, life, community and more aspects of the human experience are delved into and in such a way that any person of any religion or even irreligious, can find useful. Then the learning and practice of mindfulness in itself is so incredible that no review could do justice to this ancient and wise way of living.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16973806773908163903" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_958140859" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/958140859" />
    <a title="howl of minerva" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4475418-howl-of-minerva"><img alt="howl of minerva" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464567350p2/4475418.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/958140859?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="howl of minerva" class="user" itemprop="url" name="howl of minerva" href="/user/show/4475418-howl-of-minerva">howl of minerva</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4475418-howl-of-minerva?shelf=brain-mind">brain-mind</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4475418-howl-of-minerva?shelf=myth-religion-folklore">myth-religion-folklore</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer958140859" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11092752132511379825">A practical phenomenology of Zen consciousness (genetivus subjectivus and genetivus objectivus).<br/><br/>"He searches all around for his thought. But what thought? It is either passionate, or hateful, or confused [i.e. is bestimmt by a Grundstimmung]. What about the past, future, or present? [Zeitlichkeit/Temporalität]. What is past that is extinct, what is future that has not yet arrived and the present has no stability. For thought, Kasyapa, cannot be apprehended, inside, or outside, or in between both</span>
  <span id="freeText11092752132511379825" style="display:none">A practical phenomenology of Zen consciousness (genetivus subjectivus and genetivus objectivus).<br /><br />"He searches all around for his thought. But what thought? It is either passionate, or hateful, or confused [i.e. is bestimmt by a Grundstimmung]. What about the past, future, or present? [Zeitlichkeit/Temporalität]. What is past that is extinct, what is future that has not yet arrived and the present has no stability. For thought, Kasyapa, cannot be apprehended, inside, or outside, or in between both. For thought is immaterial, invisible, nonresisting, inconceivable, unsupported and homeless. Thought has never been seen by any of the Buddhas, nor do they see it, nor will they see it. And what the Buddhas never see, how can that be an observable process, except in the sense that dharmas proceed by the way of mistaken perception? Thought is like a magical illusion; by an imagination of what is actually unreal [uneigentlich?] it takes hold of a manifold variety of rebirths. A thought is like the stream of a river, without any staying power; as soon as it is produced it breaks up and disappears. A thought is like the flame of a lamp, and it proceeds through causes and conditions. A thought is like lightning, it breaks up in a moment and does not stay on... <br /><br />"Searching for thought all around, he does not see it within or without. He does not see it in the skandhas [kategorien?], or in the elements, or in the sense-fields. Unable to see thought, he seeks to find the trend of thought and asks himself: whence is the genesis of thought? And it occurs to him that "where there is an object, there thought arises." [Intentionalität]. Is then the thought one thing, and the object another? No, what is the object, just that is the thought. If the object were one thing and the thought another, then there would be a double state of thought. So the object itself is just thought. [Epoché; Einklammerung]. Can then thought review thought? No, thought cannot review thought. As the blade of a sword cannot cut itself, so a thought cannot see itself. Moreover, vexed and pressed hard on all sides, thought proceeds, without any staying power, like a monkey or like the wind. It ranges far, bodiless, easily changing, agitated by the objects of sense, with the six sense-fields for its sphere, connected with one thing after another. The stability of thought, its one-pointedness, its immobility, its undistraughtness [Gelassenheit], its one-pointed calm, its nondistraction, that is on the other hand called mindfulness as to thought."<br /><br />-from Śikṣāsamuccaya </span>
  <a data-text-id="11092752132511379825" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_434798760" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/434798760" />
    <a title="Vikkat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1105074-vikkat"><img alt="Vikkat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1269081822p2/1105074.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/434798760?book_show_action=true">Oct 15, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vikkat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vikkat" href="/user/show/1105074-vikkat">Vikkat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1105074-vikkat?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer434798760" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7478613580408465045">While I was reading this (excellent) book, it struck me how much of it recognize from, well, life. Some of the most composed and peaceful people I know already seem to be following TNH's directions, altough they wouldn't call it zen or meditation. This might sound funny, but the most vivid example is washing dishes, brought up by author so often. My stepfather, who is a very wise man, would never go to sleep when there's dishes in the sink, and always takes his sweet time washing them, as if it </span>
  <span id="freeText7478613580408465045" style="display:none">While I was reading this (excellent) book, it struck me how much of it recognize from, well, life. Some of the most composed and peaceful people I know already seem to be following TNH's directions, altough they wouldn't call it zen or meditation. This might sound funny, but the most vivid example is washing dishes, brought up by author so often. My stepfather, who is a very wise man, would never go to sleep when there's dishes in the sink, and always takes his sweet time washing them, as if it was the most important job of his day. My best friend (who recommended this book to me) shares her kitchen with so many people that washing dishes indeed becomes a time-consuming chore, yet she always does it gracefully and with full attention, and that's probably the reason why I can visit her and drink out of the same mug as I did many years ago without it being broken somewhere along the way. For me it's especially hard to be mindful, and take it slow, because I was taught that no matter what I do, I could always put this one hour to a better use. I mean, sometimes I catch myself regretting the fact that I cannot watch a movie, read a book, do pushups and knit at the same time. I spend so much time planning and scolding myself that in the end nothing much gets done. But, thanks to this book, I might be on my way to recovery.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7478613580408465045" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/774914-sheila?shelf=self-improvement-health">self-improvement-health</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer881670233" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3000795318391559081">What a fascinating, thought provoking book. I am very interested in this idea of "mindfulness" and am now trying to put into practice many of the ideas the author of this book suggests. I am finding doing this helps my stress too. If I can focus on the moment, if I can control my mind and just enjoy the moment, the present, what I am actually doing, it does make me calmer and less frazzled. It is a great idea! Who would have thought that it could be calming to wash the dishes, or fold the laundr</span>
  <span id="freeText3000795318391559081" style="display:none">What a fascinating, thought provoking book. I am very interested in this idea of "mindfulness" and am now trying to put into practice many of the ideas the author of this book suggests. I am finding doing this helps my stress too. If I can focus on the moment, if I can control my mind and just enjoy the moment, the present, what I am actually doing, it does make me calmer and less frazzled. It is a great idea! Who would have thought that it could be calming to wash the dishes, or fold the laundry? <br /><br />The only thing that was a bit "strange" for me with the book was some of the ideas for meditation on death, on dead bodies. That seemed a little "out there" for me, so I am not going to go that far, but for the general idea, I found this great! I am going to investigate this idea of mindfulness further. <br /><br />For anyone curious on what "mindfulness" is, this book seems to be an excellent introduction to the topic. The author is a Buddhist monk, but he is very open to and accepting of all religions, and doesn't just focus on Buddhism. <br /><br />Thank you Sapfo for the recommendation! <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3000795318391559081" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer484335402" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9237131942343250965">I remember when I was a student. The rules were to simply meditate and that was it! The same with Hindu Yoga. The same with all Eastern paths. Then I stumbled on this book. It was wonderful. Did you know that you can put in as little as 10 minutes a day of sitting meditation and then apply this mindfulness of breath to "washing the dishes"? Later, apply mindfulness (being aware) to taking a bath. To eating. Well, you will meditate now for 1 hour a day. In fact, Buddhist Masters state that minful</span>
  <span id="freeText9237131942343250965" style="display:none">I remember when I was a student. The rules were to simply meditate and that was it! The same with Hindu Yoga. The same with all Eastern paths. Then I stumbled on this book. It was wonderful. Did you know that you can put in as little as 10 minutes a day of sitting meditation and then apply this mindfulness of breath to "washing the dishes"? Later, apply mindfulness (being aware) to taking a bath. To eating. Well, you will meditate now for 1 hour a day. In fact, Buddhist Masters state that minfulness in daily life is more important than the actual sitting meditation! Buy this book and become a 16-hour a day meditator. Doing "TV meditation", "conversation meditation", "telephone meditation", "cooking meditation". you name it. Nhat Hanh gives a variety of sitting meditations. Pick the one that feels right. Then do the meditation in daily life. Right now, I am doing "write a review meditation". I wasn't at first. But I am presently. The feel of everything that is happening in the present. Good luck.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9237131942343250965" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1895429760" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1895429760" />
    <a title="Leslie Reese" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6584206-leslie-reese"><img alt="Leslie Reese" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325635683p2/6584206.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1895429760?book_show_action=true">Feb 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leslie Reese" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leslie Reese" href="/user/show/6584206-leslie-reese">Leslie Reese</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/32803226-the-miracle-of-mindfulness-gift-edition">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1895429760" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17402638935759330038">In 1974 Thich Nhat Hanh wrote a personal letter of encouragement to Brother Quang and the student workers in the School of Youth for Social Service in South Vietnam. <b>The Miracle of Mindfulness</b> is that letter. The tone is very soothing, wise, and loving, and now that I have been practicing meditation for a few years, I feel even more receptive to his words. I’ve chosen to keep <b>The Miracle of Mindfulness</b> by my bedside, to read from it often, on any day, at any time of day. This first encounter has</span>
  <span id="freeText17402638935759330038" style="display:none">In 1974 Thich Nhat Hanh wrote a personal letter of encouragement to Brother Quang and the student workers in the School of Youth for Social Service in South Vietnam. <b>The Miracle of Mindfulness</b> is that letter. The tone is very soothing, wise, and loving, and now that I have been practicing meditation for a few years, I feel even more receptive to his words. I’ve chosen to keep <b>The Miracle of Mindfulness</b> by my bedside, to read from it often, on any day, at any time of day. This first encounter has made me more aware of myself often being in a hurry and caught up in distractions: the way I’ve often eaten an apple, taking its smell, taste, and texture for granted while multi-tasking, as if my world might collapse if I were to stop and focus on the joy, blessing, and nourishment of eating an apple.<br /><br />Because the edition I read is the <i>Gift Edition</i>, it also includes Thich Nhat Hanh’s calligraphy, several pages of “exercises in mindfulness;” a tribute by Jim Forest titled “Seeing With The Eyes of Compassion,” a “Selection of Buddhist Sutras,” photographs, and a chronology of Thich Nhat Han’s life.<br /><br /><i>“For beginners, I recommend the method of pure recognition: recognition without judgement. Feelings, whether of compassion or irritation, should be welcomed, recognized, and treated on an absolutely equal basis; because both are ourselves. the tangerine I am eating is me. The mustard greens I am planting are me. I plant with all my heart and mind. I clean this teapot with the kind of attention I would have were I giving the baby buddha or Jesus a bath. Nothing should be treated more carefully than anything else. In mindfulness, compassion, irritation, mustard green plant, and teapot are all sacred.”</i> (from page 61)</span>
  <a data-text-id="17402638935759330038" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I'm such a slow a reader: I borrowed this last month and I'm just about to approach the 90-page mark. This book is barely 100 pages.<br/><br/>I picked up this book because last month, I was going through a phase, or a refining fire, or a test.   <span id="freeText5024252831781386525" style="display:none">I'm in the middle of reading this. This is milestone book for me because It's the first book I borrowed from the public library. I'm such a slow a reader: I borrowed this last month and I'm just about to approach the 90-page mark. This book is barely 100 pages.<br /><br />I picked up this book because last month, I was going through a phase, or a refining fire, or a test. Someone I've known for quite some time and only recently became a friend pointed me to Pema Chondron's thoughts on shenpa and Thich Nhat Hanh is the only thing available for me to read. My delight blossomed in the first few pages as I discover that the way I think is written in this book. It's a simple read, it's simplicity provoking its readers to take pause and think about the present moment. To think about what's here and what's now.<br /><br />I'm in the middle of developing my own yoga practice and struggling to develop my own daily rituals. This book reminds me to start somewhere vital: breathing. I needed this book. Everyone should read it.</span>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2305873427?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dubzor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dubzor" href="/user/show/4449370-dubzor">Dubzor</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2305873427" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7525594411261908608">This was disappointing, as I have heard many great things about Hanh's writing. This book however is a mix of many things, and not all of it is terribly accessible to beginners, which is what the book is supposed to be about.<br/><br/>The first half of the book is a collections of letters, expanded upon, discussing Mindfulness but also talking a great deal about the importance of the breath and breathing in mindfulness. This portion of the book is the most useful, the approach of capturing ones breath and</span>
  <span id="freeText7525594411261908608" style="display:none">This was disappointing, as I have heard many great things about Hanh's writing. This book however is a mix of many things, and not all of it is terribly accessible to beginners, which is what the book is supposed to be about.<br /><br />The first half of the book is a collections of letters, expanded upon, discussing Mindfulness but also talking a great deal about the importance of the breath and breathing in mindfulness. This portion of the book is the most useful, the approach of capturing ones breath and it's important part in meditation and mindfulness is something that I haven not yet read thus far. That being said, it's not terribly deep, it's all very matter of fact and offers no real analysis of it to be had.<br /><br />After this, it's a collection of anecdotes and scripture translations. These are painfully repetitive, dense, and offer no practical interpretation, just straight translations. This portion of the book is incredibly frustrating.<br /><br />So, sadly I cannot say this I would recommend this book for beginners of the subject. His Holiness has released several books of in introductory manner that are far more concise and clear on the subject.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7525594411261908608" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Examine to see all the complexes that have arisen within you from the feeling that you are not capable of realizing success. Shed the light of interdependence on the whole matter to see that failures cannot be accounted for by your inabilities but rather by the lack of favorable conditions. See that you have no strength to shoulder these failures, that these failures are not your own self. See to it that you are free from them. Only when you can relinquish them can you really be free and no longer assailed by them."<br /><br />-p. 97</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5364458259135903223">Who knew Thich Nhat Hanh would be so brilliant, philosophical while also writing at the Everyman level? A book to savor and apply. Drawing on Tolstoy, "there is only one important time and that is now. The present moment is the only time over which we have dominion. The most important person is always the person who you are with, who is right before you'. . . . We talk about social service, service to the people, service to humanity, service for others who are far away, helping to bring peace to</span>
  <span id="freeText5364458259135903223" style="display:none">Who knew Thich Nhat Hanh would be so brilliant, philosophical while also writing at the Everyman level? A book to savor and apply. Drawing on Tolstoy, "there is only one important time and that is now. The present moment is the only time over which we have dominion. The most important person is always the person who you are with, who is right before you'. . . . We talk about social service, service to the people, service to humanity, service for others who are far away, helping to bring peace to the world -- but often we forget that it is the very people around us that we must live for first of all. If you cannot serve your wife or husband or child or parent -- how are you going to serve society?. . . . [I]f we want to put it into practice we must use the methods of mindfulness in order to seek and find the way." (p. 116 - 118) Now, on to practice the mindfulness meditations in the back of the book. Suggested companion peace: Jon Kabat-Zinn's "Coming to Our Senses"!</span>

<div id="review_29094225" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="♥ Ibrahim ♥ " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1178748-ibrahim"><img alt="♥ Ibrahim ♥ " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557598809p2/1178748.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/29094225?book_show_action=true">Aug 02, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer29094225" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2625558896977820243">I wish I had read Thây (Thích) long time ago! The only reason I didn't read him is because I was afraid of his Buddhist views while claiming to be open-minded,etc. This book is a gem and is a book to keep and refer to over and over again. It begins where one is, with basically very little knowledge of how to "breathe" and be mindful. These breathing techniques are currently being used as excellent tools by doctors and are proven to work very well in lowering blood pressure, etc. Take a look here</span>
  <span id="freeText2625558896977820243" style="display:none">I wish I had read Thây (Thích) long time ago! The only reason I didn't read him is because I was afraid of his Buddhist views while claiming to be open-minded,etc. This book is a gem and is a book to keep and refer to over and over again. It begins where one is, with basically very little knowledge of how to "breathe" and be mindful. These breathing techniques are currently being used as excellent tools by doctors and are proven to work very well in lowering blood pressure, etc. Take a look here for instance:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.huffingtonpost.com/2013/08/09/breathing-health_n_3696302.html?utm_hp_ref=mostpopular">http://www.huffingtonpost.com/2013/08...</a><br />You read the book and you come to discover that the book is so easy and it reads by itself and, most importantly, it is practical manual for daily living: learning to watch, to follow one's breath and be a master of one's one mind instead of being passively resigned to negative emotions that might assail us from time to time. We learn to enjoy what we are doing for its own sake, not as a "means" but it is both a subject and an object at the same time. For instance, I don't wash the dishes and get done so I could do something else more fun but I will enjoy what I am doing in the present moment for what it is, and so I will be washing the dishes just to wash the dishes and I will be aware of myself and what I am doing as if it is the only things in my entire life. Chapter 2 is really excellent and I read it several times because it gives gradual, basic and advanced breathing exercises. Anybody can do these exercises anytime they want and not be intimidated. He explains all that as if he is talking to you and speaking to your heart. Towards the end of the book, it has many exercises in mindfulness and a selection of Buddhist sutras so we get to taste their own texts in their own context and in the original Eastern ancient spirit. A pdf copy of this book is available for free download on <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://terebess.hu/zen/mesterek/Thich%20Nhat%20Hanh%20-%20The%20Miracle%20of%20Mindfulness.pdf">https://terebess.hu/zen/mesterek/Thic...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="2625558896977820243" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1220522428376269482">Mindfulness and meditation are, contrary to what many in the West may think, more than sitting quietly on the floor and staring into nothingness for 30 minutes a day. They are a way of life, a way of framing and contextualizing yourself, your actions and existence in a different way. <br/>Since this was originally a letter from one Buddhist monk to another, many of the things described are not viable for a Western 40+ hour workday and lifestyle. However, the principles and foundations are still valua</span>
  <span id="freeText1220522428376269482" style="display:none">Mindfulness and meditation are, contrary to what many in the West may think, more than sitting quietly on the floor and staring into nothingness for 30 minutes a day. They are a way of life, a way of framing and contextualizing yourself, your actions and existence in a different way. <br />Since this was originally a letter from one Buddhist monk to another, many of the things described are not viable for a Western 40+ hour workday and lifestyle. However, the principles and foundations are still valuable resources for everyone interested in the topic, and even small changes can make a big difference in a hectic, aggressive lifestyle.<br />The simple language betrays the depths of its content. Just like the allegorical story included in later chapters, most paragraphs are invitations to dwell on their meaning for much longer than it takes to process the mere words.<br />I am neither a religious nor a spiritual person, but I find a deep, calming value in many of the teaching and exercises described in this book and can easily appropriate them to enrich my own mindset. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1220522428376269482" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <a title="Siva Prasad. H" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Siva Prasad. H" href="/user/show/58420527-siva-prasad-h">Siva Prasad. H</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2031727139" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3652981887570786351">Miracle of mindfulness is a book which comes under one of my favourite genre, spirituality/Buddhism. And I loved it so much. It's such a short'n sweet book. It's not a complicated one, or it's not teaching us anything which is hard to digest for the normal people and for the beginners too. It's all about controlling our mind to lead a peaceful life. They're teaching how to control our mind, how to live happily, how to stay peaceful every time step by step with their personal and real life exampl</span>
  <span id="freeText3652981887570786351" style="display:none">Miracle of mindfulness is a book which comes under one of my favourite genre, spirituality/Buddhism. And I loved it so much. It's such a short'n sweet book. It's not a complicated one, or it's not teaching us anything which is hard to digest for the normal people and for the beginners too. It's all about controlling our mind to lead a peaceful life. They're teaching how to control our mind, how to live happily, how to stay peaceful every time step by step with their personal and real life examples at the end. This short book is one of the most easiest and simplest spiritual book I've ever come across in my whole life. With huge love and respect, I'm recommending this to everyone who likes reading spiritual or Buddhist books. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3652981887570786351" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1533352151" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1533352151" />
    <a title="Adam McNamara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21981770-adam-mcnamara"><img alt="Adam McNamara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543512806p2/21981770.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1533352151?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam McNamara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam McNamara" href="/user/show/21981770-adam-mcnamara">Adam McNamara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21981770-adam-mcnamara?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21981770-adam-mcnamara?shelf=self-help">self-help</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21981770-adam-mcnamara?shelf=reread">reread</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21981770-adam-mcnamara?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1533352151" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18317673847574914103">The Miracle of Mindfulness is a fantastic introduction to mindfulness. It's extremely approachable, using plain language to explain how and why to integrate mindfulness into life.<br/><br/>Exerises in Mindfulness was my favorite chapter. I had a more difficult time with the Selected Buddhist Sutras, but perhaps I wasn't yet ready for those.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('vuY+R7nP/iUMoLBcZ/Z1Xb6Gjaiu3oSl4AlADVwasszSJgYo3CqXwWt03uvsXo3ag3QhnQsLYcPN93zpt5Rgsw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ZtzsT+1yQZBVslo78nIuXEfyLdOF1qLZ/LCWLj9XxDcKHNQgiJcodDJmNIx52tbbegCB5iADR7/RTqrK1NkWSA==')})

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_7968013'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7968013-the-buddha-is-still-teaching?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Buddha Is Still Teaching: Contemporary Buddhist Wisdom<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/59705.Jack_Kornfield?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Jack Kornfield<\/a><span title=\"Goodreads Author!\">*<\/span>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.17 avg rating &mdash; 272 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2010\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer8477110165478913023\">When the Buddha set in motion the wheel of Dharma, he knew that the teaching he gave was inexhaustible—that every future generation would find its own skillful ways to convey it to the hearts and minds of those ready to hear. The Buddha Is Still Teac<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText8477110165478913023\" style=\"display:none\">When the Buddha set in motion the wheel of Dharma, he knew that the teaching he gave was inexhaustible—that every future generation would find its own skillful ways to convey it to the hearts and minds of those ready to hear. The Buddha Is Still Teaching is testimony to the fulfillment of that promise today. The selections it contains, from today’s most highly regarded contemporary Buddhist teachers, bring the Dharma eloquently to life for us in our own time, place, and culture. They demonstrate that two and a half millennia have done nothing to diminish the freshness of the Buddhist teachings, or their universal applicability to our lives. Contributors include: Ajahn Chah, Joko Beck, Sylvia Boorstein, Tara Brach, Pema Chödrön, the Dalai Lama, Ram Dass, Mark Epstein, Norman Fischer, Natalie Goldberg, Joseph Goldstein, Dilgo Khyentse, Jack Kornfield, Noah Levine, Stephen Levine, Sakyong Mipham, Sharon Salzberg, Suzuki Roshi, Robert Thurman, Thich Nhat Hanh, and Tulku Thondup. And it inspired two close friends to get together in Dharamsala for a talk about something very important to them. The friends were His Holiness the Dalai Lama and Archbishop Desmond Tutu. The subject was joy. Both winners of the Nobel Prize, both great spiritual masters and moral leaders of our time, they are also known for being among the most infectiously happy people on the planet.From the beginning the book was envisioned as a three-layer birthday cake: their own stories and teachings about joy, the most recent findings in the science of deep happiness, and the daily practices that anchor their own emotional and spiritual lives. Both the Dalai Lama and Archbishop Tutu have been tested by great personal and national adversity, and here they share their personal stories of struggle and renewal. Now that they are both in their eighties, they especially want to spread the core message that to have joy yourself, you must bring joy to others.Most of all, during that landmark week in Dharamsala, they demonstrated by their own exuberance, compassion, and humor how joy can be transformed from a fleeting emotion into an enduring way of life.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"4624648118008536428\" <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer886319956597903517"><b>Thích Nhất Hạnh</b> is a Vietnamese Buddhist monk, teacher, author, poet and peace activist who now lives in southwest France where he was in exile for many years. Born <b>Nguyễn Xuân Bảo</b>, Thích Nhất Hạnh joined a Zen (Vietnamese: Thiền) monastery at the age of 16, and studied Buddhism as a novitiate. Upon his ordination as a monk in 1949, he assumed the Dharma name Thích Nhất Hạnh. Thích is an honorary </span>
  <span id="freeText886319956597903517" style="display:none"><b>Thích Nhất Hạnh</b> is a Vietnamese Buddhist monk, teacher, author, poet and peace activist who now lives in southwest France where he was in exile for many years. Born <b>Nguyễn Xuân Bảo</b>, Thích Nhất Hạnh joined a Zen (Vietnamese: Thiền) monastery at the age of 16, and studied Buddhism as a novitiate. Upon his ordination as a monk in 1949, he assumed the Dharma name Thích Nhất Hạnh. Thích is an honorary family name used by all Vietnamese monks and nuns, meaning that they are part of the Shakya (Shakyamuni Buddha) clan. He is often considered the most influential living figure in the lineage of Lâm Tế (Vietnamese Rinzai) Thiền, and perhaps also in Zen Buddhism as a whole.</span>
  <a data-text-id="886319956597903517" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/105847-the-miracle-of-mindfulness-a-manual-on-meditation">Quotes from The Miracle of Mi...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;People usually consider walking on water or in thin air a miracle. But I think the real miracle is not to walk either on water or in thin air, but to walk on earth. Every day we are engaged in a miracle which we don't even recognize: a blue sky, white clouds, green leaves, the black, curious eyes of a child—our own two eyes. All is a miracle.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/5022-people-usually-consider-walking-on-water-or-in-thin-air">992 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Feelings, whether of compassion or irritation, should be welcomed, recognized, and treated on an absolutely equal basis; because both are ourselves. The tangerine I am eating is me. The mustard greens I am planting are me. I plant with all my heart and mind. I clean this teapot with the kind of attention I would have were I giving the baby Buddha or Jesus a bath. Nothing should be treated more carefully than anything else. In mindfulness, compassion, irritation, mustard green plant, and teapot are all sacred.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/95549-feelings-whether-of-compassion-or-irritation-should-be-welcomed-recognized">276 likes</a>
            </nobr>
